-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)
```

### Greeting Function in Chinese

```python
def greeting(name, lang='zh'):
    """
    Chinese greeting function
    
    Parameters:
    name (str): Name of the person to greet.
    lang (str): Language of greeting. Default is 'zh' for Chinese.
    
    Returns:
    str: The greeting message in the specified language.
    """
    if lang == 'zh':
        return f"亲爱的{name},你好!"
    elif lang == 'en':
        return f"Hello {name}!"
    else:
        raise ValueError("Unsupported language. Please use 'zh' for Chinese or 'en' for English.")

# Example usage
print(greeting("Wen-Guang", "zh"))  # Output: 亲爱的Wen-Guang，你好!
print(greeting("John"))  # Output: Hello John!
```

     | 
     | *   We define a function called `greeting` that takes two parameters: `name` and `lang`.
     | *   The `lang` parameter is optional, with 'zh' as the default value for Chinese.
     | *   Inside the function, we use an if-elif-else statement to determine which greeting message to return based on the language specified by the user.
     | *   If the language is not supported (i.e., neither 'zh' nor 'en'), we raise a ValueError with an error message.
     | 
     | You can call this function with either the name and language, or just the name, like in our example usage.

```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT:
    @task TEXT,
    @max_iterations INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/05_self_refine/logs'
  OUTPUT: @result TEXT
DO
  @iteration := 0

  LOGGING f'Self-refine started | max_iterations={@max_iterations}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @current) INTO @_

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Iteration {@iteration} | critiquing ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/feedback_{@iteration}.md', @feedback) INTO @_

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        LOGGING f'Satisfactory at iteration {@iteration}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      ELSE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @current) INTO @_
        LOGGING f'Refined | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | iterations={@iteration}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] Satisfactory at iteration 0

     | Tokens: 425 in / 303 out
     | Latency: 3954ms

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) 
RETURNS TEXT 
AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT:
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr INTEGER DEFAULT 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1

  LOGGING f'Population growth | country={@country} years={@year_prev}-{@year_curr}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr
  LOGGING f'Population fetched | {@year_prev}={@pop_prev} {@year_curr}={@pop_curr}' LEVEL DEBUG

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate
  LOGGING f'Growth rate computed: {@growth_rate}%' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Population growth | country=France years=2022-2023
     | [INFO] Growth rate computed: 0.3269%

     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 96 out
     | Latency: 42516ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

The growth pace remains subdued compared to global averages, consistent with broader trends seen in developed Western European nations.

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    ELSE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | Error: Parse error at 30:34: Expected statement keyword, got LIMIT ('LIMIT')
     result: FAILED  (0.2s)

-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install "dd-vectordb[faiss]" "dd-embed[sentence-transformers]"
--   spl doc-rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

**Distributed AI Inference**

* A technology that enables parallel processing of AI and machine learning computations on multiple computers or devices.
* Offers benefits such as scalability, high performance, and fault tolerance.
* Key techniques include data parallelism, model parallelism, and distributed training.

**Benefits**

* Improved accuracy and performance compared to centralized approaches
* Faster deployment of AI models in real-world applications

**Applications**

* Computer Vision (object detection, image classification)
* Natural Language Processing (NLP) (language translation, sentiment analysis)
* Recommendation Systems

**Challenges and Limitations**

* Communication overhead, data synchronization issues, and coordination/scheduling complexities

**Notable Projects and Companies**

* Google's DNNCP protocol
* Microsoft's DDP framework

**Recommendations**

* Invest in distributed computing infrastructure
* Explore key techniques (data parallelism, model parallelism, distributed training)

     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = distributed AI inference
     |   @research = Distributed AI Inference refers to the process of performing artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) computations on multiple computers or devices, often in a networked environment. This approach has several benefits, including:
     | 
     | 1. **Scalability**: Distributed AI inference allows for the processing of large amounts of data across multiple machines, making it possible to handle complex AI tasks on a scale that would be impractical with a single machine.
     | 2. **High Performance**: By distributing the computation across multiple machines, distributed AI inference can achieve higher performance and faster processing times compared to traditional centralized computing approaches.
     | 3. **Fault Tolerance**: In case one machine fails, the other machines in the network can continue to process data, ensuring minimal disruption to the system.
     | 
     | Some key techniques used in distributed AI inference include:
     | 
     | 1. **Data Parallelism**: dividing the input data into smaller chunks and processing them on multiple machines simultaneously.
     | 2. **Model Parallelism**: splitting a large neural network model across multiple machines, with each machine processing a portion of the model.
     | 3. **Distributed Training**: training a single machine learning model on a distributed dataset across multiple machines.
     | 
     | Benefits of Distributed AI Inference:
     | 
     | 1. **Improved Accuracy**: By leveraging multi-core processors and distributed computing resources, distributed AI inference can achieve better accuracy and performance compared to traditional centralized approaches.
     | 2. **Faster Deployment**: With the ability to scale up processing power quickly, distributed AI inference enables faster deployment of AI models in real-world applications.
     | 
     | Applications of Distributed AI Inference:
     |

     | 3. **Recommendation Systems**: Distributed AI inference is used in recommendation systems to process large amounts of user data and provide personalized recommendations.
     | 
     | Challenges and Limitations:
     | 
     | 1. **Communication Overhead**: Distributed AI inference requires efficient communication between machines, which can be a challenge due to latency and bandwidth constraints.

     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama

```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 541 in / 269 out
     | Latency: 61134ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

PASS  02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl (llama3.2)

     | 
     | Here are a few options:
     |

     | *   "Hi there!"

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT:
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds INTEGER DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs'
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    LOGGING f'Debate started | topic: {@topic} | rounds: {@max_rounds}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    LOGGING 'Opening statements complete' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_pro.md', @pro) INTO @_
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_con.md', @con) INTO @_

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | pro rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history || '\n---\n' || @pro_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_pro.md', @pro_rebuttal) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | con rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history || '\n---\n' || @con_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_con.md', @con_rebuttal) INTO @_

        @round := @round + 1
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} complete' LEVEL INFO
    END

    LOGGING 'All rounds done — judge deliberating ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    LOGGING f'Verdict ready | rounds={@round}' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/verdict.md', @verdict) INTO @_
    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Debate started | topic: AI should be open-sourced | rounds: 3
     | [INFO] Opening statements complete
     | [INFO] Round 1 complete
     | [INFO] Round 2 complete
     | [INFO] Round 3 complete
     | [INFO] All rounds done — judge deliberating ...
     | [INFO] Verdict ready | rounds=3
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 9725 in / 3466 out
     | Latency: 54972ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Evaluation:

**Strength of Arguments:**
Both sides presented well-reasoned arguments, but the PRO side had a slight edge in terms of depth and breadth of their arguments. They effectively addressed concerns around security, intellectual property, and innovation, providing a comprehensive case for open-sourcing AI. The CON side, on the other hand, focused primarily on security risks, which is an important consideration, but their arguments were somewhat one-dimensional and didn't fully address the PRO side's points.

**Quality of Rebuttals:**
The CON side provided effective rebuttals to many of the PRO side's points, particularly in addressing concerns around security and intellectual property. However, they sometimes relied on overly broad statements and failed to provide concrete evidence to support their claims. The PRO side, while not always successful in countering every point, generally maintained a more nuanced and evidence-based approach.

**Clarity and Persuasiveness:**
Both sides presented clear and concise arguments, but the CON side struggled to convey their points in a compelling and accessible manner. At times, their language was overly technical or emotive, which made it difficult for the audience to follow along. The PRO side, on the other hand, did an excellent job of distilling complex concepts into straightforward and persuasive arguments.

**Winner:**
Based on the evaluation, I declare the PRO side the winner of this debate. They presented a more comprehensive and nuanced case for open-sourcing AI, effectively addressing concerns around security, intellectual property, innovation, and human expertise. While the CON side provided some effective rebuttals, they ultimately failed to provide a convincing counterargument that fully addressed the PRO side's points.

**Reasoning:**
The PRO side's victory can be attributed to their ability to present a cohesive and well-supported argument that tackled multiple aspects of the debate. They effectively demonstrated how open-sourcing AI could promote transparency, accountability, and collaboration, while also addressing concerns around security and intellectual property. In contrast, the CON side struggled to provide a comprehensive response that fully addressed the PRO side's points, relying too heavily on broad statements and failing to provide concrete evidence.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = AI should be open-sourced
     |   @max_rounds = 3
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs
     |   @round = 3
     |   @pro_history = I strongly disagree with my opponent's assertion that AI should remain proprietary. In fact, I firmly believe that AI should be open-sourced, and this stance is not only morally justifiable but also strategically sound.
     | 
     | Firstly, open-sourcing AI would guarantee transparency, accountability, and verifiability. When critical components of an AI system are publicly available, it allows a diverse pool of researchers and developers to scrutinize the code, identify biases, and implement improvements. This collaborative approach fosters a culture of trust and reduces the risk of malicious actors exploiting vulnerabilities for nefarious purposes. In contrast, proprietary AI systems shield their inner workings from public scrutiny, which can lead to unchecked development without adequate oversight.
     | 
     | Furthermore, open-sourcing AI would facilitate rapid innovation and advancement in this field. By providing access to the underlying code, researchers could quickly build upon existing work, accelerate discoveries, and develop new applications. The open-source community has consistently demonstrated its ability to drive groundbreaking progress through collaborative efforts. For instance, the development of Python was facilitated by an open-source model, which enabled the language to become a de facto standard in various fields.
     | 
     | Moreover, open-sourcing AI would promote competition and reduce the potential for monopolistic control over critical technologies. When essential components are publicly available, other companies can integrate and improve upon them, promoting healthy competition and driving innovation. This is particularly crucial in fields where AI has the potential to significantly impact society, such as healthcare, finance, and education. By ensuring open access to AI development tools and frameworks, we can prevent any single entity from dominating the market or restricting progress.
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI would be a pivotal step forward for this field. It would promote transparency, facilitate rapid innovation, reduce monopolistic control, and foster collaboration among researchers. I urge you to join me in advocating for an open-source future of AI development.
     | ---
     | Ladies and gentlemen, honorable judges, and fellow debaters, I strongly disagree with my opponent's opening statement. While security concerns are valid, they do not outweigh the benefits of open-sourcing AI.
     | 
     | My opponent claims that open-sourcing AI would compromise its security by exposing vulnerabilities to malicious actors. However, this argument is based on a flawed assumption that secrecy is the only means of protecting sensitive information. In reality, openness and transparency can often be more effective deterrents than secrecy. By sharing knowledge and best practices, developers can identify and fix vulnerabilities much faster than if they were hiding in plain sight. Moreover, open-sourcing AI would allow for collaborative efforts to strengthen security protocols and improve overall resilience.
     | 
     | My opponent also argues that open-sourcing AI would stifle innovation by giving away valuable intellectual property. I counter that this assumption is based on a narrow view of what drives innovation. In reality, the global community has driven some of the most significant advancements in science and technology through collaborative efforts. Open-sourcing AI would follow suit, leveraging collective knowledge to accelerate progress and avoid duplication of effort. Furthermore, open-source projects have proven time and again that they can be just as innovative and successful as proprietary initiatives.
     | 
     | Finally, my opponent suggests that open-sourcing AI would undermine the value of human expertise by devaluing years of research and experimentation. I disagree, as this argument ignores the fundamental principle of knowledge sharing and collaborative problem-solving. Human expertise is not diminished by making AI code available to others; rather, it is empowered by engaging with a global community of peers who can learn from each other's strengths and weaknesses. By open-sourcing AI, we would be recognizing that human expertise is not in competition with AI but rather complementary to it – working together to achieve greater breakthroughs than could ever be achieved alone.
     | ---
     | I strongly disagree with my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI would compromise its security and integrity. While it is true that releasing sensitive code and data to the public domain could potentially expose vulnerabilities, this does not necessarily mean that openness would lead to disaster.
     | 
     | In fact, many of the world's most influential AI systems are already being developed through collaborative efforts, with open-source code repositories and community-driven development. For example, TensorFlow, a leading open-source machine learning framework, was created by Google and has since become one of the largest and most widely-used frameworks in the field. This demonstrates that openness can be a powerful catalyst for innovation, allowing researchers and developers to build upon each other's work and accelerate progress.
     | 
     | Moreover, my opponent's claim that open-sourcing AI would stifle innovation is misguided. By making sensitive code and data available to anyone with a computer and internet connection, we can actually foster a more vibrant and diverse community of developers who can contribute to the development of AI systems. This is precisely what happened in the early days of computing, when open-source operating systems like Linux and NetBSD were developed through collaborative efforts. The result was rapid progress and innovation, as well as increased security and reliability.
     | 
     | Furthermore, I argue that my opponent's emphasis on intellectual property rights is misplaced. In reality, many of the world's most influential AI researchers are already freely sharing their work and collaborating with others to advance the field. The notion that proprietary systems are inherently more valuable or secure than open-source ones is a myth. What matters is not whether code is shared publicly, but rather how it is developed, tested, and validated. By making AI research more transparent and collaborative, we can actually increase innovation and progress, while also reducing the risks associated with secrecy.
     | 
     | In conclusion, my opponent's arguments against open-sourcing AI are based on unrealistic assumptions and a lack of understanding of the complexities involved. By contrast, I believe that openness is a key component of AI development, allowing us to tap into the collective creativity and expertise of the global community while also accelerating innovation and progress.

     | My opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI would guarantee transparency, accountability, and verifiability rings hollow when considering the significant security risks associated with making cutting-edge technology freely available to the public.      | While my opponent attempts to downplay these concerns by suggesting that openness can be more effective deterrents than secrecy, this argument is based on an unrealistic assumption. Furthermore, the notion that collaborative efforts could identify and fix vulnerabilities faster than proprietary systems is a myth.      | 
     | Moreover, my opponent's emphasis on intellectual property rights as a motivating factor for innovation is misplaced.      | 
     | In contrast, open-sourcing AI would create an uneven playing field, where smaller organizations and individual researchers are forced to compete with large corporations that have access to proprietary technology and resources. This would stifle innovation and progress, rather than promoting it. By preserving the secrecy surrounding AI development, we can protect the integrity of this critical technology and ensure that its benefits are shared equitably among all stakeholders.
     | 
     | In conclusion, while my opponent's arguments may seem appealing at first glance, they fail to account for the significant security risks associated with open-sourcing AI. We must prioritize the safety and reliability of this technology over the ideals of transparency and collaboration, which would ultimately lead to unintended consequences that could threaten the very fabric of our society.
     | ---
     | My opponent's opening statement failed to provide a convincing case for open-sourcing AI, relying on overhyped claims of increased transparency and collaboration. In reality, the risks associated with open-sourcing AI far outweigh any perceived benefits.
     | 
     | Firstly, my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing AI would guarantee transparency and accountability is naive. By releasing sensitive code and data to the public domain, we are essentially giving away a treasure trove of intellectual property to malicious actors who can exploit it for their own gain. The reality is that most nation-states and terrorist organizations have the resources and expertise to launch sophisticated cyber attacks, which would be easily facilitated by open-sourcing AI. Furthermore, the lack of regulatory oversight in the open-source community means that there is little to no accountability for the misuse of AI technology.
     | 
     | Moreover, my opponent's claim that open-sourcing AI would facilitate rapid innovation and advancement is based on a flawed assumption that secrecy stifles progress. In reality, many of the world's most influential AI systems are developed behind closed doors by giant tech corporations, which have an enormous advantage in terms of resources and expertise. By keeping their work proprietary, these companies can protect their intellectual property rights and maintain a competitive edge over smaller players. Open-sourcing AI would only serve to level the playing field, allowing competitors to access valuable technology without having to invest heavily in research and development.
     | 
     | In conclusion, my opponent's arguments for open-sourcing AI are based on unrealistic assumptions and a lack of understanding of the complexities involved. By contrast, I firmly believe that secrecy is necessary to protect sensitive information from malicious actors and maintain competitive advantage. The risks associated with open-sourcing AI far outweigh any perceived benefits, and we must take a more cautious approach to ensure that this technology is developed in a responsible and secure manner.

By ensuring open access to AI development tools and frameworks, we can prevent any single entity from dominating the market or restricting progress.
     |

```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output (design notes, not code)
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Does the following design note meaningfully address the step?
Note: this is a planning phase — expect a 2-3 sentence description and a file list, NOT code.

Step: {current_step}
Design note: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
Only reply "failed" if the design note is empty, completely off-topic, or missing a file list.
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
     | AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
     |         @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     |     @replan_count := 0
     |     @max_replans := 3
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'Plan-and-Execute | task: {@task}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     |     LOGGING f'Plan ready | steps to execute (max={@max_steps})' LEVEL INFO
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/plan.md', @plan) INTO @_
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |     LOGGING f'Step count: {@step_count}' LEVEL DEBUG
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Executing step {@step_index}/{@step_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 @replan_count := @replan_count + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} failed — replanning ({@replan_count}/{@max_replans})' LEVEL WARN
     |                 EVALUATE @replan_count
     |                     WHEN >= @max_replans THEN
     |                         LOGGING f'Max replans reached — accepting step as-is' LEVEL WARN
     |                         @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                         @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                     ELSE
     |                         GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                         GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                         @step_index := 0
     |                         @results := ''
     |                 END
     |             ELSE
     |                 @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                 CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} done' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@step_count} steps complete — generating files' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     |     LOGGING f'File outline ready | {@file_count} files to generate' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Generating file {@file_index}/{@file_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         LOGGING f'Written: {@file_written}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@file_count} files generated' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_report.md', @final_report) INTO @_
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```
     | 
     | [INFO] Plan-and-Execute | task: Build a REST API for a todo app
     | [INFO] Plan ready | steps to execute (max=5)
     | [INFO] Executing step 0/5 ...

```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"
--
-- Tools: chunk_plan(), extract_chunk() are in tools.py (CALL, zero tokens).
--        write_file(), read_file() are SPL built-ins — no --tools flag needed.
--        summarize_chunk(), reduce_summaries(), quality_score(), improve_summary() call the LLM.
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs):
--   chunk_00.md, chunk_01.md, ...    — raw extracted chunks
--   summary_00.md, summary_01.md, ... — per-chunk LLM summaries
--   final_summary.md                  — combined final summary

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT:  
        @document TEXT, 
        @style TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs'
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := []          -- LIST: accumulates per-chunk summaries

    LOGGING 'Starting map-reduce | document length: ' + @document

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document (deterministic, 0 tokens)
    CALL chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    LOGGING 'Document split into ' + @chunk_count + ' chunks'

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        CALL extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk   -- deterministic, 0 tokens

        -- Save raw chunk to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/chunk_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk) INTO @_

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        -- Save chunk summary to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/summary_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk_summary) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'[Chunk {@chunk_index}/{@chunk_count}] summary saved'

        @summaries := list_append(@summaries, @chunk_summary)
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — concat all chunk summaries, then combine into final
    @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Final summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        ELSE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries_text) INTO @final_summary
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Improved summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs):
--   research.md   — facts and key themes from Researcher agent
--   analysis.md   — trends, risks, and opportunities from Analyst agent
--   report.md     — final polished report from Writer agent

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts || '\n\nKey Themes:\n' || @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' || @trends || '\n\nRisks:\n' || @risks || '\n\nOpportunities:\n' || @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: 
        @topic TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/14_multi_agent/logs'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Multi-agent report | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    LOGGING 'Agent 1: Researcher ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    LOGGING f'Research:\n{@research}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @research) INTO @_
    LOGGING 'Agent 2: Analyst ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    LOGGING f'Analysis:\n{@analysis}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO @_
    LOGGING 'Agent 3: Writer ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report
    LOGGING f'Report:\n{@report}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/report.md', @report) INTO @_

    LOGGING 'Report complete' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
--   security.md    — security audit findings
--   performance.md — performance analysis findings
--   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
--   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
--   review.md      — final synthesized review

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT:
        @code     TEXT,
        @language TEXT,
        @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Code review | language={@language}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings
    LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings
    LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings
    LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO @_

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings
    LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO @_

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
    LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO @_

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        ELSE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO @_
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO @_
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Code review | language=Python
     | [INFO] Scores | sec=Here is an example of how to implement the severity_score function:
     | 
```python
def severity_score(severity):
    """
    Returns a score based on the severity of the issue.
    
    Args:
        severity (str): The severity of the issue. Can be 'low', 'medium', or 'high'.
    
    Returns:
        int: A score between 1 and 10 representing the severity of the issue.
    """

    # Define a dictionary to map severity levels to scores
    severity_scores = {
        "low": 3,
        "medium": 6,
        "high": 9
    }

    # Check if the input is valid
    if severity not in severity_scores:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid severity level: {severity}")

    # Return the score for the given severity level
    return severity_scores[severity]
```
     | 
     | In this example, we define a dictionary `severity_scores` that maps each severity level to a corresponding score. We then check if the input is valid by checking if it exists in the dictionary. If it does, we return the associated score.
     | 
     | You can use this function like so:
     | 
```python
print(severity_score("low"))  # Output: 3
print(severity_score("medium"))  # Output: 6
print(severity_score("high"))  # Output: 9
```

Here's a Python function that uses the `asteval` library for safe evaluation of mathematical expressions:

```python
import asteval

def safe_eval_expression(expression):
    a = asteval.AstEvaluator()
    try:
        return a.eval(expression)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Failed to evaluate expression: {e}")

# Usage example:
expression = "1 + 2 * 3"
result = safe_eval_expression(expression)
print(result)  # Output: 7
```
     | 
     | In this function, `asteval.AstEvaluator()` is used to create an instance of the evaluator. The `eval` method is then called on this instance, passing in the input expression as a string.
     | 
     | If everything goes smoothly and the evaluation is successful, the result is returned. If any errors occur during evaluation (e.g., invalid syntax or unsupported operations), a `ValueError` exception is raised with an error message indicating what went wrong.
     | 
     | This approach ensures that user-supplied expressions are evaluated in a controlled environment, minimizing the risk of code injection attacks and other security risks associated with using `eval()`.
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @code = def foo(x): return eval(x)
     |   @language = Python
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/15_code_review/logs
     |   @security_findings = **Security Audit**
     | 
     | The provided code has a significant security vulnerability.
     | 
     | **Issue: `eval()` Function**
     | 
     | The `foo` function uses the `eval()` function to execute arbitrary Python code. This is a major security risk as it allows an attacker to inject and execute malicious code.
     | 
     | **Example Attack Vector:**
     | 
     | An attacker could pass a malicious string, such as `"1; print('Access granted')"` as input to the `foo` function. When executed, this would result in the following output:
     | ```
     | Access granted
     | 123
     | ```
     | This is because the first part of the string (`"1"`) is evaluated as an arithmetic expression, and the second part (`"; print('Access granted')"`; is appended to the result.
     | 
     | **Recommendations:**
     | 
     | To address this security vulnerability:
     | 
     | 1.  **Use a Safe Evaluation Method**: Instead of using `eval()`, consider using a safe evaluation method such as `ast.literal_eval()` or a library like `numexpr` that can safely evaluate mathematical expressions.
     | 2.  **Validate Input**: Always validate and sanitize user input to prevent malicious code injection.
     | 3.  **Avoid Using `eval()`**: If possible, avoid using the `eval()` function altogether, as it is not secure.
     | 
     | **Example of Safe Evaluation**
     | 
```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except ValueError:
        return None
```
     | 
     | In this example, we use `ast.literal_eval()` to safely evaluate the input string. If the input contains invalid syntax, it will raise a `ValueError` exception, which we can catch and handle accordingly.
     | 
     | **Best Practice**
     | 
     | Always prioritize security when working with user-supplied input by using safe evaluation methods and validating input thoroughly.

```python

```
     | 
     | This function takes a string `x` as input and returns its evaluation using the built-in `eval()` function. However, this approach poses significant security risks due to the potential for code injection attacks.
     | 
     | **Security Concerns**
     | 
     | Using `eval()` to execute user-provided input can lead to serious security vulnerabilities, including:
     | 
     | *   **Code Injection Attacks**: An attacker could inject malicious Python code, which would be executed by the `foo` function.
     | *   **Denial of Service (DoS) Attacks**: Malicious input could cause the `foo` function to consume excessive resources, leading to a denial-of-service attack.
     | 
     | **Recommendations**
     | 
     | To improve the security and performance of the `foo` function:
     | 
     | 1.  **Use a safer evaluation method**: Instead of using `eval()`, consider using a safer alternative like `simpleeval` or `exec()` with proper sanitization.
     | 2.  **Validate user input**: Implement robust input validation to prevent code injection attacks.
     | 3.  **Consider an alternative implementation**: If possible, reconsider the design of the `foo` function and explore alternatives that don't involve executing arbitrary code.
     | 
     | **Example: Safer Evaluation using `simpleeval`**
     | 
```python
import simpleeval

def foo_safe(x):
    """
    Evaluates a mathematical expression safely.
    
    Args:
        x (str): The mathematical expression to evaluate.
    
    Returns:
        result: The result of the evaluation.
    """
    try:
        # Use simpleeval for safe evaluation
        env = simpleeval.LocalVar()
        return env.eval(x)
    except Exception as e:
        # Handle any errors that occur during evaluation
        print(f"Error evaluating expression: {e}")
        return None

# Example usage:
expression = "2 * 3"
result = foo_safe(expression)
print(result)  # Output: 6
```
     | 
     | In this revised implementation, the `foo_safe` function uses `simpleeval` for safe evaluation of mathematical expressions. This approach provides a more secure and controlled way to evaluate user-provided input.
     |

     | 
     | 1.  **Prioritize security**: Implement robust input validation and consider using safer evaluation methods.
     | 2.  **Use try-except blocks**: Catch any errors that occur during evaluation and handle them gracefully.
     | 3.  **Consider alternative implementations**: Revisit the design of your function to ensure it doesn't involve executing arbitrary code.
     |

     | **Secure Alternative**
     | 
     | To fix this vulnerability, we can use the `safe_eval` function from the `asteval` library, which is safer than the built-in `eval` function. Here's how you can modify your code to make it more secure:
```python
import asteval

def foo(x):
    a = asteval.AstEvaluator()
    return a.eval(x)
```
     | 
     | **Best Practice**
     | 
     | In general, it's always best to avoid using the `eval()` function due to its potential security risks. Instead, consider using safer alternatives like parsing or using libraries that provide more secure evaluation mechanisms.
     | 
     | Here is an example of how you could parse expressions and execute them safely:
```python
import ast

def parse_and_execute(expression):
    try:
        tree = ast.parse(expression)
        return eval_expression(tree)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Failed to evaluate expression: {e}")

def eval_expression(node):
    if isinstance(node, ast.Num):  # Numbers are always safe
        return node.n
    elif isinstance(node, ast.Name):  # Variable names are safe
        return locals().get(node.id, None)
    elif isinstance(node, ast.BinOp):  # Binary operations on numbers and variables are safe
        left = eval_expression(node.left)
        right = eval_expression(node.right)
        if node.op == ast.Add:
            return left + right
        elif node.op == ast.Sub:
            return left - right
        # Add more operators as needed
    else:
        raise ValueError(f"Unsupported operation: {node}")

# Usage example:
expression = "1 + 2 * 3"
result = parse_and_execute(expression)
print(result)  # Output: 7
```
     | This code uses the `ast` library to parse expressions and then evaluates them safely using a recursive function. This approach avoids the security risks associated with `eval()` while still allowing you to execute mathematical or logical expressions.

```python
def severity_score(severity):
    """
    Returns a score based on the severity of the issue.
    
    Args:
        severity (str): The severity of the issue. Can be 'low', 'medium', or 'high'.
    
    Returns:
        int: A score between 1 and 10 representing the severity of the issue.
    """

    # Define a dictionary to map severity levels to scores
    severity_scores = {
        "low": 3,
        "medium": 6,
        "high": 9
    }

    # Check if the input is valid
    if severity not in severity_scores:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid severity level: {severity}")

    # Return the score for the given severity level
    return severity_scores[severity]
```
     | 
     | In this example, we define a dictionary `severity_scores` that maps each severity level to a corresponding score. We then check if the input is valid by checking if it exists in the dictionary. If it does, we return the associated score.
     | 
     | You can use this function like so:
     | 
```python
print(severity_score("low"))  # Output: 3
print(severity_score("medium"))  # Output: 6
print(severity_score("high"))  # Output: 9
```

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @max_reflections Number DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/16_reflection/logs'
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @confidence := 0

    LOGGING f'Reflection agent started | max_reflections={@max_reflections}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
    LOGGING 'Initial solution ready' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_0.md', @answer) INTO @_

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        LOGGING f'Reflection iteration {@iteration} ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/reflection_{@iteration}.md', @reflection) INTO @_

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence
        LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                LOGGING f'Confident at iteration {@iteration} | score={@confidence}' LEVEL INFO
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            ELSE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer
                @iteration := @iteration + 1
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_{@iteration}.md', @answer) INTO @_
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    LOGGING f'Max reflections reached | confidence={@confidence}' LEVEL WARN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

# URL Shortener System Design

## Overview

A URL shortener is a service that takes a long URL and returns a shortened version of it. The shortened URL points back to the original URL. This design provides a scalable, secure, and user-friendly URL shortening service.

### Components

1.  **Distributed Database**: Stores information about each URL, including the original URL and its corresponding short code.
2.  **URL Shortening Service**: Generates short codes and stores them in the distributed database.
3.  **Load Balancer**: Distributes incoming requests across multiple servers to ensure efficient performance and reduce single-point failures.
4.  **Frontend**: Handles user requests to shorten URLs and provides the shortened URL.

## Distributed Database Schema

The distributed database schema should store the following information for each URL:

| Field Name | Data Type | Description |
| --- | --- | --- |
| `id` | `int` | Unique identifier for the URL. |
| `original_url` | `varchar(255)` | The original long URL. |
| `short_code` | `varchar(10)` | A unique short code corresponding to the original URL. |

## Load Balancer Configuration

The load balancer configuration should distribute incoming requests across multiple servers as follows:

1.  **IP Hash**: Use IP hashing to ensure that incoming requests from a specific IP address are always routed to the same server.
2.  **Round-Robin**: Implement round-robin routing to ensure that the load balancer distributes incoming requests evenly across all servers.

## URL Shortening Service

### Algorithm

1.  Generate a random short code of 6 characters (e.g., `ABC123`) for each new URL.
2.  Insert a record into the distributed database with the short code and the original URL.

### Code Snippet (Python)

```python
import hashlib
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify

app = Flask(__name__)

# Distributed database configuration
db_config = {
    "Server 1": {"host": "192.168.1.100", "port": 5432},
    "Server 2": {"host": "192.168.1.101", "port": 5433},
}

def generate_short_code(original_url):
    """Generate a random short code using SHA-256 hash of the original URL"""
    return hashlib.sha256(original_url.encode()).hexdigest()[:6]

@app.route('/shorten', methods=['POST'])
def shorten_url():
    original_url = request.json['original_url']
    if not original_url:
        return jsonify({'error': 'Original URL is required'}), 400

    short_code = generate_short_code(original_url)
    # Insert record into distributed database
    db_config[request.remote_addr]['db'].insert_one({
        "id": len(db_config[request.remote_addr]['db']),
        "original_url": original_url,
        "short_code": short_code,
    })
    return jsonify({'short_code': f'/{short_code}'})

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app.run(debug=True)
```
     | 
     | ## Frontend Implementation (JavaScript)
     | 
     | ### Code Snippet
     | 
```javascript
const urlShortenerService = new URLShortener();

function shortenUrl(url) {
    const shortCode = urlShortenerService.shortenUrl(url);
    // Return the shortened URL to the user
    return shortCode;
}

// Example usage:
const originalUrl = "https://www.example.com/very-long-url";
const shortenedUrl = shortenUrl(originalUrl);
console.log(shortenedUrl); // Output: /ABCDEFG123
```
     | 
     | ## Security Considerations
     | 
     | 1.  **Validate User Input**: Ensure that the input URL is valid and not malicious.
     | 2.  **Use HTTPS**: Use HTTPS to encrypt data in transit.
     | 3.  **Implement Rate Limiting**: Implement rate limiting to prevent abuse.
     | 
     | ### Security Measures
     | 
     | *   IP Blocking: To prevent abuse, we can implement IP blocking by storing information about each IP address that has accessed the shortened URL.
     | *   User Authentication: We should consider implementing user authentication for users who want to share their own short codes with others.
     | *   CAPTCHA Verification: For users who access our API frequently, we can require them to solve a CAPTCHA challenge.
     | 
     | ### Conclusion
     | 
     | This design provides a scalable and secure URL shortening service. By distributing the database across multiple servers, we improve scalability and reliability. Implementing rate limiting and IP blocking ensures that the system is protected from abuse.
