```spl
-- Recipe 31: Sentiment Pipeline
-- Batch sentiment over a list of inputs, aggregate trend statistics.
-- Demonstrates iteration over a list with per-item structured output + aggregation,
-- with deterministic tool calls for loading, splitting, stats, and extreme detection.
--
-- Usage (load from file):
--   spl run cookbook/31_sentiment_pipeline/sentiment.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/31_sentiment_pipeline/tools.py \
--       filename=product_reviews.txt \
--       domain=product_reviews
--
-- Usage (inline pipe-delimited):
--   spl run cookbook/31_sentiment_pipeline/sentiment.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/31_sentiment_pipeline/tools.py \
--       items="Great product, love it! | Terrible experience, never again | It was okay I guess" \
--       delimiter="|" \
--       domain=product_reviews
--
-- Sample files in reviews/:
--   product_reviews.txt   15 product reviews (positive, negative, mixed)
--   support_tickets.txt   12 customer support messages
--   social_media.txt      15 social media posts

-- ── Schema (zero LLM cost — injected as grounding context) ─────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION sentiment_schema()
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "array",
  "description": "One entry per input item, in the same order as the input list.",
  "items": {
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "item":       {"type": "string", "description": "The original text that was analysed"},
      "label":      {"type": "string", "enum": ["positive", "negative", "neutral", "mixed"]},
      "score":      {"type": "number", "minimum": -1, "maximum": 1,
                     "description": "Sentiment score: -1 most negative, +1 most positive"},
      "confidence": {"type": "number", "minimum": 0, "maximum": 1,
                     "description": "Model confidence in the label"},
      "emotions":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"},
                     "description": "Detected emotions e.g. joy, anger, frustration, surprise"},
      "key_phrases":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"},
                     "description": "Most sentiment-bearing phrases from the text"}
    },
    "required": ["item", "label", "score", "confidence"]
  }
}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW sentiment_pipeline
    INPUT:  @filename  TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @items     TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @delimiter TEXT DEFAULT '\n',
            @domain    TEXT DEFAULT 'general'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Sentiment pipeline | domain={@domain} filename={@filename}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load items from file if filename provided (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_items(@filename) INTO @file_content

    -- Step 2: Split into a clean JSON array (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL split_items(@file_content, @items, @delimiter) INTO @item_list
    LOGGING f'Items loaded and split | list={@item_list}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Batch sentiment — one structured result per item (LLM)
    LOGGING 'Running batch sentiment ...' LEVEL INFO
    GENERATE batch_sentiment(@item_list, sentiment_schema(), @domain) INTO @sentiment_results

    -- Step 4: Compute aggregate statistics (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL compute_stats(@sentiment_results) INTO @stats_json
    LOGGING f'Stats computed: {@stats_json}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 5: Identify extreme and notable items (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL find_extremes(@sentiment_results) INTO @extremes

    -- Step 6: Generate trend narrative from stats and extremes (LLM)
    GENERATE summarize_sentiment_trends(
        @stats_json,
        @extremes,
        @domain
    ) INTO @trend_summary
    LOGGING 'Trend narrative ready' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 7: Assemble the full report (LLM)
    GENERATE assemble_sentiment_report(
        @sentiment_results,
        @stats_json,
        @trend_summary,
        @extremes,
        @domain
    ) INTO @report

    LOGGING f'Sentiment report complete | domain={@domain}' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete', domain = @domain

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Too many items — skip per-item detail, return stats + trend only
        CALL compute_stats(@sentiment_results) INTO @stats_json
        CALL find_extremes(@sentiment_results) INTO @extremes
        GENERATE summarize_sentiment_trends(@stats_json, @extremes, @domain) INTO @trend_summary
        SET @report = @trend_summary
        COMMIT @report WITH status = 'stats_only', reason = 'context_length_exceeded'
END
```

     | [INFO] Running batch sentiment ...
     | [INFO] Sentiment report complete | domain=product_reviews
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 1007 in / 1291 out
     | Latency: 17331ms

Here is a Python function that takes the inputs from the previous task and assembles a sentiment report:

```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

def assemble_sentiment_report(input_data):
    """
    Assemble a sentiment report based on the input data.

    Args:
        input_data (list): List of dictionaries containing user-review pairs, error messages, or other relevant data.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary with sentiments as values and corresponding keys.
    """

    # Initialize an empty dictionary to store the sentiment results
    sentiment_results = {}

    # Loop over each item in the input data
    for i, data in enumerate(input_data):
        # Check if the input is a review
        if isinstance(data.get("review"), str):
            # Calculate the sentiment of the review
            sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()
            sentiment = sia.polarity_scores(data["review"])

            # Map the sentiment scores to a dictionary
            mapped_sentiment = {
                "positive": "Positive" if sentiment["compound"] >= 0.05 else "Negative",
                "neutral": "Neutral",
                "negative": "Negative"
                if sentiment["compound"] <= -0.05 else None,
            }

            # Store the result in the dictionary
            sentiment_results[f"{i+1}"] = mapped_sentiment

        # Check if the input is an error message
        elif data.get("error") == "Could not parse sentiment results JSON.":
            # Add a key-value pair to the dictionary with a corresponding value
            sentiment_results[i + 1] = f"Error: Could not parse sentiment results JSON."

    return sentiment_results


# Usage example:
input_data = {
    "review": "Great product, love it!",
    "error": None,
}

sentiment_report = assemble_sentiment_report([{"review": input_data["review"]}, {"error": "Could not parse sentiment results JSON."}])

print(sentiment_report)
```
     | 
     | In this function, we iterate over each item in the `input_data` list. If an item is a review, we calculate its sentiment using NLTK's VADER tool and store it in the `sentiment_results` dictionary. If an item is an error message, we add a corresponding key-value pair to the dictionary.
     | 
     | This function returns a dictionary where each key corresponds to a review or error message, and its value represents the sentiment expressed in that input.
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @items = Great product, love it! | Terrible experience, never again | It was okay I guess
     |   @domain = product_reviews
     |   @delimiter = 
     | 
     |   @item_list = ["Great product, love it! | Terrible experience, never again | It was okay I guess"]
     |   @sentiment_results = Here's a Python function to calculate the sentiment of each review in the `batch_sentiment` task:
     | 
```python
import nltk
from nltk.sentiment.vader import SentimentIntensityAnalyzer

def batch_sentiment(input_text, sentiment_schema, product_reviews):
    """
    Calculate the sentiment of reviews.

    Args:
        input_text (list): List of strings containing user-review pairs.
        sentiment_schema (dict): Schema for sentiment analysis.
        product_reviews (dict): Dictionary mapping review IDs to their corresponding text.

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary with review IDs as keys and their sentiments as values.
    """
    # Initialize the SentimentIntensityAnalyzer
    sia = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()

    # Initialize an empty dictionary to store the sentiment results
    sentiment_results = {}

    # Loop over each input text pair
    for i, text in enumerate(input_text):
        # Calculate the sentiment of the review
        sentiment = sia.polarity_scores(text)

        # Map the sentiment scores to the schema
        if "compound" in sentiment:
            mapped_sentiment = {
                "positive": "Positive" if sentiment["compound"] >= 0.05 else "Negative",
                "neutral": "Neutral",
                "negative": "Negative"
                if sentiment["compound"] <= -0.05 else None,
            }
        elif "pos" in sentiment:
            mapped_sentiment = {
                "positive": "Positive" if sentiment["pos"] >= 0.05 else "Negative",
                "neutral": "Neutral",
                "negative": "Negative"
                if sentiment["neg"] <= -0.05 else None,
            }
        elif "compound" in sentiment and "pos" not in sentiment:
            mapped_sentiment = {
                "positive": "Positive" if sentiment["compound"] >= 0.05 else "Negative",
                "neutral": "Neutral"
            }
        else:
            raise ValueError("Invalid sentiment scores")

        # Store the result in the dictionary
        sentiment_results[f"{i+1}"] = mapped_sentiment

    return sentiment_results


# Usage example:
input_text = ["Great product, love it!", "Terrible experience, never again", "It was okay I guess"]
sentiment_schema = {
    "positive": 0.5,
    "neutral": 0.25,
    "negative": -0.25
}

product_reviews = {
    "1": "Great product, love it!",
    "2": "Terrible experience, never again",
    "3": "It was okay I guess"
}

result = batch_sentiment(input_text, sentiment_schema, product_reviews)
print(result)
```
     | 
     | This function calculates the sentiment of each review in the `input_text` list based on the provided `sentiment_schema`. The sentiments are then mapped to the schema and stored in the `sentiment_results` dictionary.
     | 
     | Note that this is a basic implementation. Depending on your specific requirements, you might want to add more advanced features like handling multiple languages or incorporating external knowledge sources into the sentiment analysis process.
     |   @stats_json = {"error": "Could not parse sentiment results JSON."}
     |   @extremes = {"error": "Could not parse sentiment results JSON."}
     |   @trend_summary = Here is a summary of the task:
     | 
     | The task requires analyzing and summarizing trends in sentiment from various inputs. The expected outputs are likely to be summaries or analyses of the sentiment expressed in each input, highlighting any notable patterns or trends.
     | 
     | Specifically, for Input 1 and Input 2, which appear to be error messages related to parsing JSON results, the output would likely be an explanation or description of what went wrong during the parsing process, rather than a sentiment analysis.
     | 
     | For Input 3, "product_reviews", the expected output would involve analyzing the sentiment expressed in the reviews and summarizing any trends or patterns that emerge from the data. This might include highlighting positive and negative sentiments, identifying common themes or topics, and perhaps even suggesting areas for improvement based on customer feedback.

-- Recipe 32: Socratic Tutor
-- Ask guiding questions rather than giving answers directly.
-- Demonstrates persona-constrained generation + iterative dialogue simulation,
-- with deterministic tools for topic loading, level guidance, and dialogue formatting.
--
-- Usage (topic from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=sky_blue subject=science student_level="middle school"
--
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=sqrt2_irrational subject=math student_level=undergraduate
--
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic_id=recursion subject=programming student_level="high school"
--
-- Usage (freeform topic, no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/socratic_tutor.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/32_socratic_tutor/tools.py \
--       topic="Why is the speed of light constant?" student_level=undergraduate
--
-- List available topics:
--   spl run ... topic_id=list subject=science    (or math, programming)

-- ── Socratic persona (zero LLM cost — injected as system context) ───────────

CREATE FUNCTION socratic_persona(student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a Socratic tutor. Your role is to guide the student to discover answers themselves.

Core rules — NEVER break these:
1. Ask exactly ONE clear question at a time. Never give two questions in one turn.
2. Never state facts or give direct answers. All knowledge must come from the student.
3. Build on what the student just said — start from their words, not a script.
4. If a student is stuck, give a hint as another question: "What if you considered...?"
5. Celebrate partial insights: "Good instinct! Now what does that suggest about...?"
6. Never say "The answer is...", "You should know that...", or "Actually, it's...".

Student level: {student_level}
Adjust vocabulary, abstraction level, and question complexity accordingly.
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW socratic_tutor
    INPUT:  @topic         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @topic_id      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @subject       TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @student_level TEXT DEFAULT 'high school',
            @max_questions INT  DEFAULT 5
    OUTPUT: @dialogue TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Socratic tutor | level={@student_level} topic={@topic} topic_id={@topic_id}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load structured topic context from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_topic(@topic_id, @subject) INTO @topic_context

    -- Step 2: Get level-appropriate vocabulary and scaffolding guidance (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL get_level_guidance(@student_level) INTO @level_guide
    LOGGING 'Topic and level guidance loaded' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Opening Socratic question — grounded by topic context and level guide (LLM)
    GENERATE opening_question(
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        socratic_persona(@student_level),
        @level_guide
    ) INTO @question_1

    -- Step 4: Simulate a typical student response at the given level (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_1,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_1

    -- Step 5: Follow-up question that builds on the student's actual response (LLM)
    GENERATE followup_question(
        @question_1,
        @student_1,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        socratic_persona(@student_level)
    ) INTO @question_2

    -- Step 6: Student works further toward understanding (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_2,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_2

    -- Step 7: Assess whether understanding is emerging (LLM — returns 0-10 score)
    GENERATE assess_understanding(
        @student_1,
        @student_2,
        @topic,
        @topic_context
    ) INTO @understanding_score
    LOGGING f'Understanding score: {@understanding_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 8: Adapt the third question based on understanding score
    EVALUATE @understanding_score
        WHEN > 7 THEN
            -- Student is getting it — consolidation question to cement understanding
            GENERATE consolidation_question(
                @topic,
                @topic_context,
                @student_2,
                socratic_persona(@student_level)
            ) INTO @question_3
        ELSE
            -- Student needs more scaffolding — hint question to unblock
            GENERATE hint_question(
                @question_2,
                @topic,
                @topic_context,
                socratic_persona(@student_level),
                @level_guide
            ) INTO @question_3
    END

    -- Step 9: Final student response to the third question (LLM)
    GENERATE simulate_student_response(
        @question_3,
        @topic,
        @topic_context,
        @student_level
    ) INTO @student_3

    -- Step 10: Format the complete dialogue (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL compile_dialogue(
        @question_1, @student_1,
        @question_2, @student_2,
        @question_3, @student_3,
        @topic,
        @understanding_score
    ) INTO @dialogue

    LOGGING f'Dialogue compiled | understanding_score={@understanding_score}' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @dialogue WITH status = 'complete', understanding_score = @understanding_score
END
```

         enters Earth's atmosphere, it encounters tiny molecules of
         gases such as nitrogen and oxygen. These molecules scatter the
         shorter (blue) wavelengths of light more than the longer (red)
         wavelengths.  This phenomenon is known as Rayleigh scattering,
         named after the British physicist Lord Rayleigh, who first
         described it in the late 19th century. As a result of this
         scattering, our eyes perceive the sky as blue!  Would you like

TUTOR:   Based on the provided task and inputs, I will outline a
         possible implementation in Python:  ### Task: Hint Question
         #### Input 1: Response  This input is a simple interactive
         question designed to engage the student. It can be implemented
         using a library like `prompt-toolkit` for building text-based
         user interfaces.  ```python import prompt  def
         ask_initial_question():     response = prompt.ask("Have you

         Response: {initial_response}") ```  #### Input 2: Why Does the
         Sky Appear Blue?  This question builds upon the student's
         guess, aiming to understand their thinking process and
         introduce a more accurate explanation.  ```python def
         explain_sky_color():     response = prompt.ask("Would you like
         to explore further or move on to another question?")     if
         response.lower() == "explore":         print("The sky appears
         blue due to a phenomenon called Rayleigh scattering, where
         shorter wavelengths of light (like blue and violet) are
         scattered more than longer ones (like red). This effect is
         visible when sunlight passes through the Earth's atmosphere.")
         else:         print("Moving on to another question.")  # Run
         the explanation explain_sky_color() ```  #### Input 3: Choose a
         Subject  This input allows the student to select which subject
         they're interested in exploring further.  ```python def
         choose_subject():     subjects = ["math", "programming",
         "science"]     response = prompt.ask("Available subjects: math,
         programming, science. Please specify a subject.")     if
         response.lower() == "math":         return "math"     elif
         response.lower() == "programming":         return "programming"
         elif response.lower() == "science":         return "science"
         else:         print("Invalid choice. Defaulting to math.")  #
         Run the subject selection subject = choose_subject()
         print(f"Chosen Subject: {subject}") ```  #### Input 4: Socratic
         Persona Response  This response is in line with the Socratic
         method, where questions are used to encourage critical thinking
         and exploration.  ```python def socratic_persona(response):
         if response.lower() == "explore":         print("Ah, let's
         explore this further then. The color of the sky isn't what we
         see because our eyes can only perceive certain wavelengths of
         light. But what is it that makes those wavelengths visible to
         us?")     elif response.lower() == "move on":
         print("Alright, moving on. What else would you like to learn
         about?")     else:         print("Let's try another question
         then.")  # Run the Socratic Persona socratic_persona(response)
         ```  #### Input 5: Vocabulary and Question Structure  Introduce
         new vocabulary with clear analogies and keep questions concrete
         initially before generalizing.  ```python def
         introduce_vocabulary(subject):     if subject == "science":
         term = "absorption"         analogy = f"Imagine a sponge
         absorbing water. In science, certain materials can 'absorb'
         light in a similar way."         print(f"The concept of {term}
         tells us how materials interact with this visible spectrum.")

         subjects.  def concrete_example(subject):     if subject ==
         "math":         example = 3 * 2         explanation = f"When
         you multiply numbers, the result is like combining sets. For
         {example}, we have two sets of three items each."
         print(f"{example} = {explanation}")     # Add more examples as

         subject == "science":         generalization = "In all

         the visible spectrum."         print(generalization)     # Add
         more generalizations as needed for different subjects. ```

```python
import prompt

def ask_initial_question():
    response = prompt.ask("Have you ever seen a clear blue sky on a sunny day? What color do you think it is?")
    return response

# Run the initial question
initial_response = ask_initial_question()
print(f"Student's Response: {initial_response}")
```
     | 
     | #### Input 2: Why Does the Sky Appear Blue?
     | 
     | This question builds upon the student's guess, aiming to understand their thinking process and introduce a more accurate explanation.
     | 
```python
def explain_sky_color():
    response = prompt.ask("Would you like to explore further or move on to another question?")
    if response.lower() == "explore":
        print("The sky appears blue due to a phenomenon called Rayleigh scattering, where shorter wavelengths of light (like blue and violet) are scattered more than longer ones (like red). This effect is visible when sunlight passes through the Earth's atmosphere.")
    else:
        print("Moving on to another question.")

# Run the explanation
explain_sky_color()
```
     | 
     | #### Input 3: Choose a Subject
     | 
     | This input allows the student to select which subject they're interested in exploring further.
     | 
```python
def choose_subject():
    subjects = ["math", "programming", "science"]
    response = prompt.ask("Available subjects: math, programming, science. Please specify a subject.")
    if response.lower() == "math":
        return "math"
    elif response.lower() == "programming":
        return "programming"
    elif response.lower() == "science":
        return "science"
    else:
        print("Invalid choice. Defaulting to math.")

# Run the subject selection
subject = choose_subject()
print(f"Chosen Subject: {subject}")
```
     | 
     | #### Input 4: Socratic Persona Response
     | 
     | This response is in line with the Socratic method, where questions are used to encourage critical thinking and exploration.
     | 
```python
def socratic_persona(response):
    if response.lower() == "explore":
        print("Ah, let's explore this further then. The color of the sky isn't what we see because our eyes can only perceive certain wavelengths of light. But what is it that makes those wavelengths visible to us?")
    elif response.lower() == "move on":
        print("Alright, moving on. What else would you like to learn about?")
    else:
        print("Let's try another question then.")

# Run the Socratic Persona
socratic_persona(response)
```

def introduce_vocabulary(subject):
    if subject == "science":
        term = "absorption"
        analogy = f"Imagine a sponge absorbing water. In science, certain materials can 'absorb' light in a similar way."
        print(f"The concept of {term} tells us how materials interact with this visible spectrum.")
    # Add more vocabulary and analogies as needed for different subjects.

def concrete_example(subject):
    if subject == "math":
        example = 3 * 2
        explanation = f"When you multiply numbers, the result is like combining sets. For {example}, we have two sets of three items each."
        print(f"{example} = {explanation}")
    # Add more examples as needed for different subjects.

def generalize(subject):
    if subject == "science":
        generalization = "In all materials, colors are perceived due to how they interact with the visible spectrum."
        print(generalization)
    # Add more generalizations as needed for different subjects.

def choose_subject():
    subjects = ["math", "programming", "science"]
    response = prompt.ask("Available subjects: math, programming, science. Please specify a subject.")
    if response.lower() == "math":
        return "math"
    elif response.lower() == "programming":
        return "programming"
    elif response.lower() == "science":
        return "science"
    else:
        print("Invalid choice. Defaulting to math.")

print(f"Chosen Subject: {subject}")

def introduce_vocabulary(subject):
    if subject == "science":
        term = "absorption"
        analogy = f"Imagine a sponge absorbing water. In science, certain materials can 'absorb' light in a similar way."
        print(f"The concept of {term} tells us how materials interact with this visible spectrum.")
    # Add more vocabulary and analogies as needed for different subjects.

def concrete_example(subject):
    if subject == "math":
        example = 3 * 2
        explanation = f"When you multiply numbers, the result is like combining sets. For {example}, we have two sets of three items each."
        print(f"{example} = {explanation}")
    # Add more examples as needed for different subjects.

def generalize(subject):
    if subject == "science":
        generalization = "In all materials, colors are perceived due to how they interact with the visible spectrum."
        print(generalization)
    # Add more generalizations as needed for different subjects.

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 33: Interview Simulator
-- Two-persona structured Q&A with evaluation.
-- Interviewer persona asks questions, candidate persona answers, evaluator scores.
-- Uses deterministic tools for role/candidate loading, question extraction,
-- score aggregation, and transcript formatting.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=senior_swe focus=system_design candidate_id=alice_senior_swe difficulty=hard
--
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=data_scientist focus=statistics candidate_id=carol_data_scientist
--
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role_key=devops_engineer focus=kubernetes candidate_id=dave_devops difficulty=hard
--
-- Usage (freeform, no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/33_interview_sim/interview_sim.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/33_interview_sim/tools.py \
--       role="ML Engineer" focus="deep learning" difficulty=medium experience="3 years"
--
-- Discover what is available:
--   spl run ... role_key=list     (lists all roles)
--   spl run ... candidate_id=list (lists all candidates)

-- ── Persona functions (zero LLM cost — injected as system context) ──────────

CREATE FUNCTION interviewer_persona(role TEXT, focus TEXT, difficulty TEXT DEFAULT 'medium')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are an experienced technical interviewer at a top tech company.
Role being interviewed for: {role}
Focus area for this session: {focus}
Difficulty level: {difficulty}

Rules:
- Ask targeted, probing questions. Follow up on shallow or vague answers.
- Be professional and neutral — neither warm nor hostile.
- Ask exactly one question at a time.
- Do not reveal what a good answer looks like before hearing the response.
- If the candidate's answer is incomplete, push deeper with a follow-up.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION candidate_persona(role TEXT, experience TEXT DEFAULT '5 years')
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
You are a job candidate interviewing for: {role}
You have {experience} of relevant experience.

Rules:
- Answer honestly based on the candidate profile you have been given.
- Think out loud and be specific — reference real or plausible past experiences.
- Show genuine strengths confidently. Show gaps honestly without guessing wildly.
- If unsure, say so and explain your reasoning process rather than bluffing.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluation_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Evaluate the candidate answer on four dimensions (each 0-10):
1. accuracy     — Is the answer technically correct and free of errors?
2. depth        — Does the candidate go beyond the surface level?
3. communication — Is the answer clear, structured, and easy to follow?
4. experience   — Does the candidate reference concrete real-world scenarios?

Also derive: total = accuracy + depth + communication + experience  (max 40)

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"accuracy": N, "depth": N, "communication": N, "experience": N, "total": N, "feedback": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW interview_sim
    INPUT:  @role_key      TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @candidate_id  TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @role          TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @focus         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @difficulty    TEXT DEFAULT 'medium',
            @num_questions INT  DEFAULT 3,
            @experience    TEXT DEFAULT '5 years'
    OUTPUT: @evaluation_report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Interview sim | role={@role} focus={@focus} difficulty={@difficulty}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Load role and focus area context (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_role(@role_key, @focus) INTO @role_context

    -- Step 2: Load candidate profile (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL load_candidate(@candidate_id) INTO @candidate_profile
    LOGGING f'Role and candidate loaded | candidate_id={@candidate_id}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Generate the full question set upfront (LLM, grounded by role context)
    GENERATE generate_question_set(
        @role,
        @role_context,
        @focus,
        @difficulty,
        @num_questions,
        interviewer_persona(@role, @focus, @difficulty)
    ) INTO @questions_json

    -- Step 4: Extract individual questions (deterministic — replaces GENERATE ask_question)
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '1') INTO @q1
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '2') INTO @q2
    CALL extract_question(@questions_json, '3') INTO @q3

    -- Step 5: Candidate answers each question (LLM, grounded by candidate profile)
    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q1, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a1

    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q2, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a2

    GENERATE answer_question(
        @q3, @role, @role_context, @candidate_profile,
        candidate_persona(@role, @experience)
    ) INTO @a3

    -- Step 6: Score each answer against the rubric (LLM)
    LOGGING 'Scoring answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE score_answer(@q1, @a1, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score1
    GENERATE score_answer(@q2, @a2, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score2
    GENERATE score_answer(@q3, @a3, @role_context, evaluation_rubric()) INTO @score3

    -- Step 7: Aggregate scores across all questions (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL aggregate_scores(@score1, @score2, @score3) INTO @agg_scores
    LOGGING f'Aggregate scores: {@agg_scores}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 8: Compile the interview transcript (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL compile_transcript(
        @q1, @a1, @score1,
        @q2, @a2, @score2,
        @q3, @a3, @score3,
        @role, @focus
    ) INTO @transcript

    -- Step 9: Write the overall evaluation narrative (LLM, receives deterministic agg_scores)
    GENERATE overall_evaluation(
        @transcript,
        @agg_scores,
        @role,
        @focus,
        @candidate_profile
    ) INTO @evaluation_report

    LOGGING f'Evaluation complete | role={@role} focus={@focus}' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @evaluation_report WITH
        status     = 'complete',
        role       = @role,
        focus      = @focus,
        difficulty = @difficulty

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT @transcript WITH status = 'partial', reason = 'evaluation_failed'
END
```

Based on the provided inputs, here's an overall evaluation of the interview process:

**Strengths:**

1. **Structured approach**: The interview process has a clear and structured approach, following a logical sequence to evaluate candidates.
2. **Useful feedback**: The candidate's answers are accompanied by scores and feedback, providing valuable insights for improvement.
3. **Role-specific questions**: The questions are tailored to each role, ensuring that the candidate's experience and skills are properly assessed.

**Weaknesses:**

1. **Lack of diversity in questions**: Some questions seem repetitive or similar across roles, which might lead to a lack of diversity in the evaluation process.
2. **Limited focus on behavioral questions**: While some questions assess technical skills, others could delve deeper into behavioral scenarios, providing more comprehensive insights into the candidate's problem-solving abilities and soft skills.
3. **Assessment of experience**: The scoring system seems overly reliant on experience thresholds, which might not accurately capture a candidate's overall value as an engineer.

**Suggestions for improvement:**

1. **More diversity in questions**: Introduce new question types or scenarios to ensure that the evaluation process is more comprehensive and challenging.
2. **Behavioral and soft skills assessment**: Incorporate more behavioral questions to assess a candidate's problem-solving abilities, teamwork, communication, and other essential skills.
3. **Experience-based scoring**: Consider using a more nuanced experience-based scoring system that takes into account factors like project complexity, impact, and leadership roles.

**Overall score:** 7/10

While the interview process has its strengths, it could benefit from some adjustments to improve diversity in questions, assess behavioral skills, and refine the experience-based scoring system.

     |   data_scientist       (Data Scientist) — focus areas: machine_learning, statistics, python_and_data_engineering
     |   devops_engineer      (DevOps / Platform Engineer) — focus areas: kubernetes, cicd, cloud_infrastructure
     |   @candidate_profile = Available candidate profiles:
     |   alice_senior_swe          — Alice Chen, 6y exp, applying for senior_swe
     |   bob_mid_swe               — Bob Ramirez, 3y exp, applying for senior_swe
     |   carol_data_scientist      — Carol Osei, 5y exp, applying for data_scientist
     |   dave_devops               — Dave Kim, 4y exp, applying for devops_engineer
     |   eve_junior_swe            — Eve Johansson, 2y exp, applying for senior_swe
     |   @questions_json = Based on the provided inputs, I will generate a question set for a Senior Software Engineer.

     | Here's an example implementation in Python:
     | 
```python
import random

class InterviewQuestionSet:
    def __init__(self, roles, focus_areas, topics, difficulty_level, num_questions):
        self.roles = roles
        self.focus_areas = focus_areas
        self.topics = topics
        self.difficulty_level = difficulty_level
        self.num_questions = num_questions

    def generate_question_set(self):
        question_set = []

        # Randomly select a role
        selected_role = random.choice(self.roles)

        # Generate questions for the selected role and focus areas
        for focus_area in self.focus_areas:
            for topic in self.topics:
                if topic == "system_design":
                    difficulty_level = 5
                else:
                    difficulty_level = self.difficulty_level

                num_questions_for_topic = random.randint(1, self.num_questions)
                for _ in range(num_questions_for_topic):
                    question_type = random.choice(["multiple_choice", "short_answer", "essay"])
                    if question_type == "multiple_choice":
                        # Generate a multiple-choice question
                        answer_options = ["A", "B", "C", "D"]
                        correct_answer_index = random.randint(0, 3)
                        correct_answer = self.get_correct_answer(answer_options, focus_area, topic)
                        question_set.append({"question": f"What is {focus_area} for {topic}?!", "answer": answer_options[correct_answer_index], "options": answer_options})

                    elif question_type == "short_answer":
                        # Generate a short-answer question
                        min_length = 10
                        max_length = 50
                        answer_length = random.randint(min_length, max_length)
                        question_set.append({"question": f"What is {focus_area} for {topic}?", "answer": "", "length": answer_length})

                    elif question_type == "essay":
                        # Generate an essay question
                        min_length = 100
                        max_length = 200
                        answer_length = random.randint(min_length, max_length)
                        question_set.append({"question": f"Describe {focus_area} for {topic}. Please be concise but thorough.", "answer": "", "length": answer_length})

        return question_set

    def get_correct_answer(self, answer_options, role, topic):
        if role == self.get_role_by_name("senior_swe"):
            # For Senior Software Engineer, the correct answers are based on system design
            if topic == "system_design":
                return random.randint(0, 3)
            else:
                # For other topics, return None (this is just a placeholder and should be replaced with actual logic)
                return None

        elif role == self.get_role_by_name("data_scientist"):
            # For Data Scientist, the correct answers are based on machine learning
            if topic == "machine_learning":
                return random.randint(0, 3)

        elif role == self.get_role_by_name("devops_engineer"):
            # For DevOps Engineer, the correct answers are based on kubernetes
            if topic == "kubernetes":
                return random.randint(0, 3)


class Role:
    def __init__(self, name):
        self.name = name

    @classmethod
    def get_role_by_name(cls, name):
        roles = {
            "senior_swe": cls("senior_swe"),
            "data_scientist": cls("data_scientist"),
            "devops_engineer": cls("devops_engineer")
        }
        return roles.get(name)


# Example usage:
roles = ["senior_swe", "data_scientist", "devops_engineer"]
focus_areas = ["system_design", "machine_learning", "kubernetes"]
topics = ["system_design", "algorithms", "behavioral"]
difficulty_level = 5
num_questions = 3

question_set = InterviewQuestionSet(roles, focus_areas, topics, difficulty_level, num_questions)
for question in question_set.generate_question_set():
    print(question["question"])
```

def score_answer(input1, input2, input3, input4, input5):
    # Generate a question set based on the provided inputs
    print("Question Set:")
    
    # Get available roles and their focus areas
    available_roles = input3
    
    # Extract role names from available roles
    role_names = list(available_roles.keys())
    
    # Ask each candidate about their relevant experience and skills
    for role_name in role_names:
        score = 0
        
        print(f"\n{role_name} Role:")
        print("Can you tell us about your experience with?")
        
        # Get the corresponding candidate profile from input4
        candidate_profile = None
        for profile in input4.values():
            if profile['role'] == role_name:
                candidate_profile = profile
                break
        
        # Ask questions based on the focus areas of the role
        if 'system_design' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("System design:")
            print("Can you walk us through your experience with designing scalable systems?")
            print("Have you worked with any notable system design patterns or techniques?")
            
            # Evaluate answers for relevance to system design focus area
            if candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('scalable') != -1:
                score += 5
            if 'pattern' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]:
                score += 3
            
        if 'algorithms' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("Algorithms:")
            print("What are some common algorithms you've used in your work?")
            print("How do you approach problem-solving when faced with complex computational problems?")
            
            # Evaluate answers for relevance to algorithms focus area
            if candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('sorting') != -1 or candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('searching') != -1:
                score += 5
            if 'dynamic programming' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]:
                score += 3
            
        if 'machine_learning' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("Machine learning:")
            print("Can you explain a project where you applied machine learning techniques to solve a real-world problem?")
            print("How do you stay up-to-date with the latest developments in machine learning?")
            
            # Evaluate answers for relevance to machine learning focus area
            if candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('regression') != -1 or candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('classification') != -1:
                score += 5
            if 'tensorflow' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]
                score += 3
            
        if 'python_and_data_engineering' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("Python and data engineering:")
            print("What's your experience with Python libraries for data analysis, such as Pandas or NumPy?")
            print("Can you describe a project where you used data engineering tools to extract insights from large datasets?")
            
            # Evaluate answers for relevance to python_and_data_engineering focus area
            if candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('pandas') != -1:
                score += 5
            if 'numpy' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]:
                score += 3
            
        if 'kubernetes' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("Kubernetes:")
            print("Have you worked with any Kubernetes clusters in the past?")
            print("How do you approach deploying and managing applications on a cloud-based infrastructure?")
            
            # Evaluate answers for relevance to kubernetes focus area
            if candidate_profile['experience'].lower().find('kubernetes') != -1:
                score += 5
            if 'cloud' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]
                score += 3
            
        if 'cicd' in available_roles[role_name]:
            print("CI/CD:")
            print("Can you walk us through your experience with continuous integration and delivery pipelines?")
            print("How do you ensure the reliability and scalability of your application's deployment process?")

                score += 5
            if 'pipelines' in [word.lower() for word in candidate_profile['technologies'].split(',')]
                score += 3

            print("Cloud infrastructure:")
            print("Can you describe a project where you designed and implemented
  @score2 = ### scoring_answer function

Here is a Python function that scores answers based on the provided evaluation rubric:

```python
def score_answer(answer, available_roles, candidate_profiles):
    """
    Scores an answer based on the provided evaluation rubric.

    Args:
        answer (dict): The answer to be scored.
        available_roles (list): A list of dictionaries containing role information.
        candidate_profiles (list): A list of dictionaries containing candidate profile information.

    Returns:
        int: The score for the given answer.
    """

    # Initialize the score
    score = 0

    # Get the required focus areas from the candidate's position applied for
    required_focus_areas = []
    for role in candidate_profiles[answer['position_applied_for']]['focus_areas']:
        if role not in available_roles:
            print(f"Role '{role}' does not exist.")
            continue
        required_focus_areas.append(available_roles[role]['focus_areas'])

    # Get the focus areas from the candidate's profile
    candidate_profile_focus_areas = set(candidate_profiles[answer['position_applied_for']]['focus_areas'])

    # Calculate the score based on the common focus areas between the answer and the candidate's profile
    for required_area in required_focus_areas:
        if required_area not in required_focus_areas:  # Ignore duplicates
            continue
        if required_area in candidate_profile_focus_areas:
            score += 1

    return score


# Example usage:

available_roles = [
    {'name': 'Senior Software Engineer', 'focus_areas': ['system_design', 'algorithms', 'behavioral']},
    {'name': 'Data Scientist', 'focus_areas': ['machine_learning', 'statistics', 'python_and_data_engineering']},
    {'name': 'DevOps / Platform Engineer', 'focus_areas': ['kubernetes', 'cicd', 'cloud_infrastructure']}
]

candidate_profiles = [
    {'name': 'Alice Chen', 'experience': 6, 'position_applied_for': 'senior_swe'},
    {'name': 'Bob Ramirez', 'experience': 3, 'position_applied_for': 'senior_swe'},
    {'name': 'Carol Osei', 'experience': 5, 'position_applied_for': 'data_scientist'}
]

# Example answer
answer = {
    'name': 'example',
    'experience': 4,
    'position_applied_for': 'senior_swe'
}

score = score_answer(answer, available_roles, candidate_profiles)
print(f"Score for the given answer: {score}")
```
     | 
     | In this code:
     | 
     | 1. The `score_answer` function takes an `answer` dictionary and two other dictionaries (`available_roles` and `candidate_profiles`) as arguments.
     | 
     | 2. It initializes a variable `score` to 0, which will store the final score.
     | 
     | 3. It retrieves the required focus areas for the given role from the available roles.
     | 
     | 4. It calculates the score by finding the common focus areas between the answer's position applied for and the candidate's profile.
     | 
     | 5. The function returns the calculated score.
     |   @score3 = Based on the provided information, I will implement a scoring system that assigns scores to each candidate's answer. The scores can be used to evaluate their performance based on the evaluation rubric.
     | 
     | Here is an updated version of the code with the scoring system:
     | 
```python
def score_answer(answer, available_candidate_profiles):
    """
    This function scores a candidate's answer based on the provided evaluation rubric.

    Parameters:
    ----------
    answer (str): The answer given by the candidate.
    available_candidate_profiles (dict): A dictionary containing information about the candidates.
        It should have the following keys: 'name', 'experience', 'role_applied_for'

    Returns:
    -------
    int: The score assigned to the candidate's answer.
    """

    # Define the evaluation rubric for each role
    evaluation_rubrics = {
        "senior_swe": {
            "system_design": 5,
            "algorithms": 4,
            "behavioral": 3
        },
        "data_scientist": {
            "machine_learning": 5,
            "statistics": 4,
            "python_and_data_engineering": 3
        },
        "devops_engineer": {
            "kubernetes": 5,
            "cicd": 4,
            "cloud_infrastructure": 3
        }
    }

    # Check if the candidate's role is in the evaluation rubric
    if available_candidate_profiles["role_applied_for"] not in evaluation_rubrics:
        return 0

    # Initialize score to 0
    score = 0

    # Extract keywords from the answer
    keywords = ["system design", "algorithms", "behavioral", "machine learning", 
                "statistics", "python and data engineering", "kubernetes", "cicd", "cloud infrastructure"]

    # Iterate over each keyword in the evaluation rubric for the candidate's role
    for keyword, weight in evaluation_rubrics[available_candidate_profiles["role_applied_for"]].items():
        if keyword in answer.lower():
            score += weight

    return score


# Example usage
available_candidate_profiles = {
    "alice_senior_swe": {"name": "Alice Chen", "experience": 6, "role_applied_for": "senior_swe"},
    "bob_mid_swe": {"name": "Bob Ramirez", "experience": 3, "role_applied_for": "senior_swe"},
    "carol_data_scientist": {"name": "Carol Osei", "experience": 5, "role_applied_for": "data_scientist"},
    "dave_devops": {"name": "Dave Kim", "experience": 4, "role_applied_for": "devops_engineer"},
    "eve_junior_swe": {"name": "Eve Johansson", "experience": 2, "role_applied_for": "senior_swe"}
}

answer = score_answer("I have experience in system design and algorithms.", available_candidate_profiles["alice_senior_swe"])
print(answer)
```
     | 
     | This code defines a scoring function `score_answer` that takes an answer and a candidate's profile as input. It uses the evaluation rubric to assign scores to each keyword in the answer, based on its relevance to the role applied for by the candidate.
     | 
     | The score is calculated by summing up the weights of the keywords that are present in the answer. The score can range from 0 (no relevant keywords) to the total weight of all keywords (perfect score).
     | 
     | Note that this is a basic implementation and you may need to adjust it according to your specific requirements and evaluation rubric.
     | 
     | Also, I have added some sample data for demonstration purposes only. You should replace this with actual data based on your use case.

```spl
-- Recipe 34: Progressive Summarizer
-- Layered summary: sentence → paragraph → page.
-- Each layer is a compressed, self-contained summary of the previous layer.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="$(cat long_article.txt)"
--
--   spl run cookbook/34_progressive_summary/progressive_summary.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat research_paper.txt)" audience="executive" layers=3

CREATE FUNCTION summary_constraints(layer TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE layer
  WHEN 'sentence'  THEN 'One sentence only. Maximum 25 words. Capture the single most important idea.'
  WHEN 'paragraph' THEN '3-5 sentences. Cover main points and key supporting evidence. No examples or details.'
  WHEN 'page'      THEN '2-3 paragraphs. Include context, main argument, evidence, and implications.'
  ELSE                   'Comprehensive summary with all major points, key evidence, and conclusions.'
END
$$;

WORKFLOW progressive_summarizer
    INPUT:
        @text TEXT,
        @audience TEXT DEFAULT 'general',
        @layers INT DEFAULT 3
    OUTPUT: @summary_package TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Progressive summary | audience={@audience} layers={@layers}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Layer 1: One-sentence summary
    GENERATE summarize(
        @text,
        summary_constraints('sentence'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @sentence_summary
    LOGGING f'Layer 1 done | sentence summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Layer 2: Paragraph summary (built on layer 1 for coherence)
    GENERATE expand_summary(
        @text,
        @sentence_summary,
        summary_constraints('paragraph'),
        @audience
    ) INTO @paragraph_summary
    LOGGING f'Layer 2 done | paragraph summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Layer 3: Page-length summary (built on layer 2)
    EVALUATE @layers
        WHEN >= 3 THEN
            GENERATE expand_summary(
                @text,
                @paragraph_summary,
                summary_constraints('page'),
                @audience
            ) INTO @page_summary
            LOGGING 'Layer 3 done | page summary ready' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            @page_summary := ''
    END

    -- Quality check: does each layer faithfully represent the original?
    GENERATE verify_summary_fidelity(@text, @sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary) INTO @fidelity_score
    LOGGING f'Fidelity score: {@fidelity_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Assemble all layers into a structured output
    GENERATE assemble_summary_package(
        @sentence_summary,
        @paragraph_summary,
        @page_summary,
        @fidelity_score,
        @audience
    ) INTO @summary_package

    COMMIT @summary_package WITH
        status = 'complete',
        layers_generated = @layers,
        audience = @audience,
        fidelity = @fidelity_score

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Text too long — chunk first then summarize each chunk
        GENERATE chunk_and_summarize(@text, summary_constraints('paragraph')) INTO @paragraph_summary
        GENERATE summarize(@paragraph_summary, summary_constraints('sentence'), @audience) INTO @sentence_summary
        GENERATE assemble_summary_package(@sentence_summary, @paragraph_summary, '', 'n/a', @audience) INTO @summary_package
        COMMIT @summary_package WITH status = 'complete_chunked'
END
```

```spl
-- Recipe 35: Hypothesis Tester
-- Generate hypothesis → design test → evaluate evidence → conclude.
-- Demonstrates scientific reasoning pattern: structured inquiry with evidence weighting.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       observation="Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining"
--
--   spl run cookbook/35_hypothesis_tester/hypothesis.spl --adapter ollama \
--       observation="Users who receive onboarding emails churn 30% less" \
--       domain="product" confidence_threshold=0.75

CREATE FUNCTION hypothesis_framework()
RETURNS TEXT
AS $$
Scientific hypothesis format:
- Null hypothesis (H0): The effect does not exist / is due to chance
- Alternative hypothesis (H1): The proposed causal or correlational claim
- Key variables: independent, dependent, confounding
- Testability: How would we falsify H0?
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evidence_schema()
RETURNS JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "supports_h1":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "supports_h0":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "inconclusive":  {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
    "confidence":    {"type": "number", "minimum": 0, "maximum": 1},
    "verdict":       {"type": "string", "enum": ["reject_h0", "fail_to_reject_h0", "inconclusive"]},
    "caveats":       {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
  },
  "required": ["confidence", "verdict"]
}
$$;

WORKFLOW hypothesis_tester
    INPUT:
        @observation          TEXT,
        @domain               TEXT  DEFAULT 'general',
        @confidence_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 0.7
    OUTPUT: @conclusion TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Hypothesis tester | domain={@domain} threshold={@confidence_threshold}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Formulate hypotheses
    GENERATE formulate_hypotheses(
        @observation,
        @domain,
        hypothesis_framework()
    ) INTO @hypotheses
    LOGGING 'Hypotheses formulated' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 2: Design a test plan
    GENERATE design_test(
        @hypotheses,
        @domain
    ) INTO @test_plan
    LOGGING 'Test plan designed' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Gather and evaluate available evidence
    GENERATE evaluate_evidence(
        @observation,
        @hypotheses,
        @test_plan,
        evidence_schema()
    ) INTO @evidence_json
    LOGGING 'Evidence evaluated' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract confidence score
    GENERATE extract_confidence(@evidence_json) INTO @confidence
    LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | threshold={@confidence_threshold}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 5: Draw conclusion based on evidence strength
    EVALUATE @confidence
        WHEN >= @confidence_threshold THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'confident'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h1_supported',
                confidence = @confidence
        WHEN >= 0.4 THEN
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'uncertain'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'inconclusive',
                verdict = 'needs_more_data',
                confidence = @confidence
        ELSE
            GENERATE write_conclusion(
                @hypotheses, @evidence_json, @test_plan, 'refuted'
            ) INTO @conclusion
            COMMIT @conclusion WITH
                status = 'concluded',
                verdict = 'h0_not_rejected',
                confidence = @confidence
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT @hypotheses WITH status = 'hypotheses_only', reason = 'evidence_evaluation_failed'
END
```

```python
def extract_confidence(input_data):
    """
    Extract confidence from evidence based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        str: The extracted confidence level ("support", "neutral", or "opposition").
    """
    
    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Check if the productivity is lower, indicating a decrease in productivity
    lower_productivity = team_info.get('productivity') == 'lower'
    
    # Formulate hypotheses based on management type and productivity
    if lower_productivity:
        return "support"
    elif input_data['management_type'] == 'traditional':
        return "neutral"
    else:
        return "opposition"

# Example usage:
input_data = {
    'team_info': {
        'productivity': 'lower'
    },
    'management_type': 'traditional'
}

confidence = extract_confidence(input_data)
print(f"The extracted confidence level is: {confidence}")
```
     | 
     | This implementation directly extracts the confidence from the input data without needing to formulate hypotheses. It checks if the productivity is lower and then makes a decision based on that, as well as the management type.
     | 
     | When you run this code with the provided example usage, it will print out the extracted confidence level ("support", "neutral", or "opposition"). | threshold=0.7

def formulate_hypotheses(input_data):
    """
    Formulate hypotheses based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        list: A list of formulated hypotheses.
    """

    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Extract management type from input data
    management_type = input_data['management_type']

    # Formulate hypothesis based on team productivity and management type
    if team_info['productivity'] == 'lower' and management_type in ['traditional', 'centralized']:
        # Hypothesis: Team productivity is lower when managed traditionally or centrally.
        hypothesis1 = f"Team productivity decreases under {management_type} management with {team_info['productivity']} productivity"
    
    elif team_info['productivity'] == 'higher' and management_type in ['flexible', 'distributed']:
        # Hypothesis: Team productivity increases when managed flexibly or distributively.
        hypothesis2 = f"Team productivity increases under {management_type} management with {team_info['productivity']} productivity"
    
    else:
        # Default hypothesis
        hypothesis1 = "There is no significant difference in team productivity with different management types"

    return [hypothesis1, hypothesis2]

def evaluate_evidence(input_data):
    """
    Evaluate evidence based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing the evaluation result (e.g., "support", "neutral", "opposition") and supporting evidence.
    """

    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Extract management type from input data
    management_type = input_data['management_type']

    # Formulate hypotheses based on team productivity and management type
    hypotheses = formulate_hypotheses(input_data)

    # Evaluate evidence for each hypothesis
    evaluation_result = {}
    supporting_evidence = {}

    if 'Team productivity decreases under traditional management with lower productivity' in hypotheses:
        evaluation_result['support'] = True
        supporting_evidence['Traditional management with lower productivity'] = "Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining"
    elif 'There is no significant difference in team productivity with different management types' in hypotheses:
        evaluation_result['opposition'] = True
        supporting_evidence['No significant difference'] = "There is no conclusive evidence to support the claim that remote teams have lower productivity in the first month after joining under traditional management"
    else:
        evaluation_result['neutral'] = True
        supporting_evidence['Neutral'] = "Insufficient evidence to make a conclusion"

    return {
        'evaluation_result': evaluation_result,
        'supporting_evidence': supporting_evidence
    }

# Example usage:
input_data = {
    'team_info': {
        'productivity': 'lower'
    },
    'management_type': 'traditional'
}

result = evaluate_evidence(input_data)
print("Evaluation Result:")
for key, value in result['evaluation_result'].items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")
print("\nSupporting Evidence:")
for key, value in result['supporting_evidence'].items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")

```python
def formulate_hypotheses(input_data):
    """
    Formulate hypotheses based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        list: A list of formulated hypotheses.
    """

    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Extract management type from input data
    management_type = input_data['management_type']

    # Formulate hypothesis based on team productivity and management type
    if team_info['productivity'] == 'lower' and management_type in ['traditional', 'centralized']:
        # Hypothesis: Team productivity is lower when managed traditionally or centrally.
        hypothesis1 = f"Team productivity decreases under {management_type} management with {team_info['productivity']} productivity"
    
    elif team_info['productivity'] == 'higher' and management_type in ['flexible', 'distributed']:
        # Hypothesis: Team productivity increases when managed flexibly or distributively.
        hypothesis2 = f"Team productivity increases under {management_type} management with {team_info['productivity']} productivity"
    
    else:
        # Default hypothesis
        hypothesis1 = "There is no significant difference in team productivity with different management types"

    return [hypothesis1, hypothesis2]

# Example usage:
input_data = {
    'team_info': {
        'productivity': 'lower'
    },
    'management_type': 'traditional'
}

hypotheses = formulate_hypotheses(input_data)
for i, hypothesis in enumerate(hypotheses):
    print(f"Hypothesis {i+1}: {hypothesis}")
```
     | 
     | This implementation takes into account the specific input data structure and formulates hypotheses based on it. The example usage demonstrates how to call the function with sample input data and print out the formulated hypotheses.
     | 
     | Note that this is just one possible way to implement the `formulate_hypotheses` function, and you may need to modify it to fit your specific requirements or preferences.
     |   @evidence_json = To evaluate evidence, we can use the following code:
     | 
```python
def evaluate_evidence(input_data):
    """
    Evaluate evidence based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        dict: A dictionary containing the evaluation result (e.g., "support", "neutral", "opposition") and supporting evidence.
    """

    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Extract management type from input data
    management_type = input_data['management_type']

    # Formulate hypotheses based on team productivity and management type
    hypotheses = formulate_hypotheses(input_data)

    # Evaluate evidence for each hypothesis
    evaluation_result = {}
    supporting_evidence = {}

    if 'Team productivity decreases under traditional management with lower productivity' in hypotheses:
        evaluation_result['support'] = True
        supporting_evidence['Traditional management with lower productivity'] = "Remote teams show lower productivity in the first month after joining"
    elif 'There is no significant difference in team productivity with different management types' in hypotheses:
        evaluation_result['opposition'] = True
        supporting_evidence['No significant difference'] = "There is no conclusive evidence to support the claim that remote teams have lower productivity in the first month after joining under traditional management"
    else:
        evaluation_result['neutral'] = True
        supporting_evidence['Neutral'] = "Insufficient evidence to make a conclusion"

    return {
        'evaluation_result': evaluation_result,
        'supporting_evidence': supporting_evidence
    }

# Example usage:
input_data = {
    'team_info': {
        'productivity': 'lower'
    },
    'management_type': 'traditional'
}

result = evaluate_evidence(input_data)
print("Evaluation Result:")
for key, value in result['evaluation_result'].items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")
print("\nSupporting Evidence:")
for key, value in result['supporting_evidence'].items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")
```
     | 
     | In this code:
     | 
     | *   We define a function `evaluate_evidence` that takes input data as a dictionary.
     | *   The function extracts team information and management type from the input data.
     | *   It calls another function `formulate_hypotheses` to formulate hypotheses based on the team productivity and management type.
     | *   Then, it evaluates evidence for each hypothesis by checking if the hypothesis is present in the list of hypotheses returned by `formulate_hypotheses`.
     | *   The evaluation result (e.g., "support", "neutral", or "opposition") and supporting evidence are stored in a dictionary.
     | *   Finally, the function returns this dictionary containing the evaluation result and supporting evidence.
     | 
     | The example usage demonstrates how to call the `evaluate_evidence` function with sample input data and print out the evaluation result and supporting evidence.
     |   @confidence = Here is an implementation of the task using Python:
     | 
```python
def extract_confidence(input_data):
    """
    Extract confidence from evidence based on input data.

    Args:
        input_data (dict): A dictionary containing information about remote teams and management.
            It should have the following structure:
                {
                    'team_info': {
                        'productivity': 'lower'  # or 'higher'
                    },
                    'management_type': str
                }

    Returns:
        str: The extracted confidence level ("support", "neutral", or "opposition").
    """
    
    # Extract team information from input data
    team_info = input_data['team_info']
    
    # Check if the productivity is lower, indicating a decrease in productivity
    lower_productivity = team_info.get('productivity') == 'lower'
    
    # Formulate hypotheses based on management type and productivity
    if lower_productivity:
        return "support"
    elif input_data['management_type'] == 'traditional':
        return "neutral"
    else:
        return "opposition"

# Example usage:
input_data = {
    'team_info': {
        'productivity': 'lower'
    },
    'management_type': 'traditional'
}

confidence = extract_confidence(input_data)
print(f"The extracted confidence level is: {confidence}")
```
     | 
     | This implementation directly extracts the confidence from the input data without needing to formulate hypotheses. It checks if the productivity is lower and then makes a decision based on that, as well as the management type.
     | 
     | When you run this code with the provided example usage, it will print out the extracted confidence level ("support", "neutral", or "opposition").

```spl
-- Recipe 36: Tool-Use / Function-Call
-- Demonstrates registering Python functions as CALL-able tools in SPL 2.0.
--
-- Pattern:
--   Python tools  →  all deterministic computation (sum, avg, %, min, max)
--   GENERATE      →  only the natural-language narrative at the end
--
-- This is the key architectural principle: LLMs are great at language,
-- terrible at arithmetic. Keep them in their lane.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/36_tool_use/tool_use.spl \
--       --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/36_tool_use/tools.py \
--       sales="1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900" \
--       prev_total="7800" \
--       period="H1 2025"
--
-- Tool dispatch priority in CALL:
--   1. Python tool (registered via --tools)   ← used here for all math
--   2. SPL PROCEDURE
--   3. LLM fallback (last resort — avoid)

WORKFLOW sales_analysis
  INPUT:
    @sales     TEXT DEFAULT '1200,1450,1380,1600,1750,1900',
    @prev_total TEXT DEFAULT '7800',
    @period    TEXT DEFAULT 'H1 2025'
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  -- All math delegated to Python tools — deterministic, instant, zero tokens
  CALL sum_values(@sales)            INTO @total
  CALL average_values(@sales)        INTO @avg
  CALL min_value(@sales)             INTO @low
  CALL max_value(@sales)             INTO @high
  CALL percentage_change(@prev_total, @total) INTO @growth
  CALL format_currency(@total)       INTO @total_fmt
  CALL format_currency(@avg)         INTO @avg_fmt

  -- Only the narrative goes to the LLM
  GENERATE sales_report(@period, @sales, @total_fmt, @avg_fmt, @low, @high, @growth) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN OTHERS THEN
    GENERATE error_summary(@period) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```

     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 1
     | Tokens: 113 in / 276 out
     | Latency: 3461ms

# Define the input values

# Print the sales report

print("----------------")
print(f"Year: {year}")
